Mixed effect modeling of occupants’ window open-
ing behaviour

==PAGE:3==
Summary
When designing and minimising energy loss from buildings, the behaviour of occupants opening and
closing of windows is an important parameter.
This report has focus on modelling and describing
windows opening in dwellings based on a huge amount of measurement data in order to develop a
precise model for the occupants behaviour. Three regression models have been developed: 1) a relative
simple Generalized Linear model, comparable to a previous published work from 2013, 2) a new Mixed
Effect model based on 1) but taking into account more parameters, and 3) a new Hidden Markov
model and its Markov Chain, optimised with the Gradient Descent method, to take into account the
time aspects for the window opening and closing situations in one of the dwellings. For all models
logistic regression have been used to explain the binary data for window actions. The models have
been compared with the previous work from 2013, and show consistent results, but the new models
provide a more detailed description of the occupants behaviour, which can be beneficially exploited by
constructional engineers and architects for future building design.

==PAGE:11==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
During the last two decades, increasing energy prices in general has implied more and more focus on
methods for saving energy in private dwellings. This both counts for the individual occupants, since
they can reduce their own energy costs, but also for the constructional engineers and architects de-
signing the dwellings for optimal energy savings. Also, reduction of CO2 emissions is an increasingly
relevant and important topic for future design of buildings.
In this aspect, window opening behaviours is important to understand and modelling, in order to ensure
better air quality in houses, for the occupants and for the health of the buildings, while having focus on
the inevitable energy loss. Fresh air from outside into the houses will often give the occupants better
health and lifestyle conditions. But energy loss cannot be avoided, since indoor heat transfer to outside
and colder air moving inside, will often result in an unnecessary increase of indoor radiator heating, and
creating local temperature variations. Therefore, building engineer and architects are showing interest
to simulate and understand the influence of occupants window behaviours, such that the buildings
energy consumption can been estimated and the building design and the expected energy consumption
can be optimised, before any part of the real construction have started.
Based on measured data for indoor and outdoor air conditions in 15 dwellings in Denmark, an article
from year 2013, [And+13] have been published about exactly this topic with the focus on estimating
a regression model for this behaviour. The original article is based on the measured data observations
and uses a Generalized Linear Logistic Regression Model for estimating the coefficients for describing
the variables influence on window opening behaviours in dwellings. The article assumes that the oc-
cupants are representative in their behaviours for the respective dwellings. The scope of the article
was limited to focus on the ventilation type and types of ownership of the dwellings, which resulted
in four combinations between rented/owned dwellings and mechanical/natural ventilation types. Thus,
the occupants individual window behaviours wasn’t investigated in that work.
Consequently, with the given same data sets, it therefore could be interesting to investigate how the
occupants behave in comparison to each other without focusing on the dwelling’s ventilation type or
ownership, only.
Therefore could an analysis of the individual dwellings effect on window opening
behaviours be interesting for understanding occupants’ behaviours. In addition the individual rooms
in dwellings variation in window actions, also could be interesting for understanding the dwellings in
details.
So, with these different approaches of what could be possible, but also interesting to focus on, the report
will mainly concentrate on the following tasks:
• To recreate the analysis of the mentioned article from 2013 [And+13] and verify the previously
found results by conducting a statistical analysis given the exact same data sets. Then we will be
sure that our new model is consistent with the previous work from 2013.
• To develop and apply a Mixed Effect model analysis to capture the individual dwellings effect on
window opening behaviours and spot differences from the results of the original article.
• To analyse and understand the time variations in individual rooms in a specific dwelling by using
a Hidden Markov model and its Markov Chain.
==PAGE:12==
2
1 Introduction
With these different approaches it is therefore expected that this project can lead to a better under-
standing of occupants’ window opening behaviours in order to develop models for building engineers,
such that new designed building can use these simulation models and take into account the given esti-
mated parameters influence. The work in this report is divided into 3 main chapters, chapter 2, 3 and
4, treating the reproduction of the original model and the two new models, respectively, as described
in the bullet section above. The different statistical methods will be described and explained in each
chapter, and every analysis and model will be using the exact same data measurement sets.
==PAGE:13==
CHAPTER 2
Article re-creation
2.1
Goal of re-creation of the article
In this chapter will the aim be to recreate the article ”Window opening behaviour modelled from
measurements in Danish dwellings”, [And+13], results and findings based on the same data sets and
methods used in the statistical programming software language R. Its objective goal to study were
to quantify the influence of environmental factors on occupants’ window opening behaviour in Danish
residential buildings.
The following recreation is therefore relevant, because it also introduces the
basic, general and underlying methods for which I want to expand.
The main reasons is therefore
that recreating the article is to verify the results and check that similar values and outputs can be
achieved, which ensures that analysing with other methods to expand the findings in the data can be
conducted. These models can thereafter be implemented in real building simulations programs, such
that the quality of the indoor climate during days over shorter or longer periods of time can be tested
and hopefully increase the validity of the simulation outcome.
2.2
Data collection
As mentioned in the original article the measurements of occupant’s window opening behaviour have
been conducted in 15 dwellings in Denmark during eight months from January to August 2008, where
indoor and outdoor environmental conditions were measured and monitored such that relation between
the behaviour of the occupants in relation to the environmental conditions were captured. Here the
different dwellings were categorised in four groups according to their ventilation type and ownership,
such that the dependencies of either natural or mechanical ventilation and a dwelling that were owned or
rented could be compared in order to investigate common patterns of behaviour. Ten of these dwellings
were rented houses and the five remaining were private apartments. A mix of these were apartments
with naturally and automatic ventilation. Furthermore a description of the residents and characteristics
of each of the dwellings can be seen in Table 2.1.

The reason for these categorised groups depended on the ownership and ventilation type were due to the
general aim of the article analysis of finding differences between dwelling. Therefore, when comparing
with these groups, it will be possible to conclude if the relation of ventilation type and ownership have
any impact on the decisions for occupants’ window opening behaviour. So therefore, data observations
have been obtained and measured by the following equipment as described:
The indoor environment measurements were carried out with Hobo U12-012 data loggers.
The CO2 concentration was measured using a Vaisala GMW22 sensor connected to the
Hobo logger. Both the CO2 sensors and the Hobo data loggers were newly calibrated from
the factory. The CO2 sensors were tested against a newly calibrated Innova multigas analyser
both before and after the measuring period. The temperature sensors in the hobo data loggers
were also tested before the measurements. These sensors for the indoor environment were
places in a distance of one meter from any radiator and further 1.8 meters height above the
floor.
The outdoor environmental variables were obtained from the Danish meteorological insti-
tute. Data from the meteorological station closest to each of the dwellings was used. The
closest meteorological stations did not measure precipitation and since local wind direction is
==PAGE:15==
2.3 Data description
5
very sensitive to local conditions it was decided not to include the direction of the wind. The
window position (open/closed) was measured using a Hobo U9 sensor. Three of the windows
were hitched in the top and tilted outwards when opening. In these cases the tilt was mea-
sured using an accelerometer (HOBO UA-004-64 Pendant G) attached to the window frame.
In this way, the opening angle of the window was measured. Generally, all measurements
were carried out in the (main) living room and the (main) bedroom in each dwelling. The
window sensors were installed on windows that inhabitants used most often when ventilating
the dwelling. The number of operable windows varied between dwellings from 1 to 4 windows
in the bedrooms and 3 to 6 windows in the living rooms. [And+13]
Further it is mentioned that given these findings above, all measurements are based on data from the
bedroom and living room. If dwellings included more then one of these rooms, then were the data a
result from the main room they uses the most with a varying frequency of window opening. This leads
to the following section where the data structure will be explained.
2.3

can be seen below in Table 2.3.

The categorical variable for ’Time of day’ is further subdivided into the following time intervals:
Night(23-6), Morning(6-9), Day(9-13), Afternoon(13-18) and Evening(18-23). The season and week
days follows the normal trends as we are used to in daily life.
Further the amount of data in total for each of the dwellings and depending on the scenario of having
a window closed or open can be observed. From Table 2.4 it is noticed that the amount of data for
scenario where the windows are open is smaller and in one case it is zero, meaning that dwelling 12 never
opens its window based on the measured data. This means that the lack of data can be an issue for
precise prediction later on, especially if the occupants window behaviours are widely different from each
other such that any regression model can’t describe the action with a statistically significant evidence.

Given all this data it is useful to have a summary statistics for the variables span. By using the following
R code below, the result from the article can be found and matched. Here the maximum, minimum,
quantiles, median and mean value be found for every variable and in this case for every categories group
of ventilation and renting type which gives the following Table 2.5 continuing the table with the last
results in Table 2.6.
# Table 6 in article 2013:
summary(dat$TempC[dat$WindowClosed==1 & dat$Group==1])
summary(dat$TempC[dat$WindowClosed==0 & dat$Group==1])
sd(dat$TempC[dat$WindowClosed==1 & dat$Group==1])
sd(dat$TempC[dat$WindowClosed==0 & dat$Group==1])
# shortcut
by(dat[, c(20,21,23,15,17,22,16,18,19)], dat[,c("WindowClosed","Group")], summary)

==PAGE:17==
2.4 Data cleaning
7
2.4
Data cleaning
When analysing the data material, it has been found the some observations had missing data observa-
tions. This is a problem for applying data cleaning in order to ensure reliable end results. Therefore
the data sets which includes NA’s in some of the variables rows have been removed, such that the data
sets are reduced in observation and ready to be used for modelling. A list of code for this data cleaning
can be seen below:
# remove NA rows
#dat <- dat[!is.na(dat$WindowClosed),]
dat <- subset(dat, !is.na(TempC) & !is.na(RHC) & !is.na(CO2C) & !is.na(LuxC) &
!is.na(Hour) & !is.na(WeekDay) & !is.na(OutdoorTemp) & !is.na(Wind) & !is.na(SolRad) &
!is.na(SolTimer) & !is.na(Room))
Listing 2: R code for removing NA data rows
Furthermore since the general linear model works most properly when the variables are normal dis-
tributed, histograms therefore have been made for each of the generated variables, to see the distribu-
tion of them. Here they are matched up with the log-transformation of the variable, to see whether it
will be a good idea to transform the individual variable. The reason for using a log-transformation is
because it’s the most common one used to deal with skewed data. The log-transformation can decrease
the variability of data and make data conform more closely to the normal distribution and thereby make
the general linear model model perform more wisely [al14]. Therefore histograms have been plotted
below, where the four last variables have been log-transformed since they were quite different from a
normal distribution.

Therefore, to deal with the huge amount of data a forward selection will be applied to a generalized
model, where the given model start with only an intercept and then add the most significant variables
which gives the following better information criteria for improving the models performance. In the
original article the Bayesian information criterion (BIC) is used, and hence will it also be used for the
following model building process.
BIC = log(n)k −2 log(L)
(2.1)
where L is the maximised value of the likelihood function of the model, x the observed data, n the
number of data points and k is the number of parameters estimated by the model.
2.6
Method: General linear model
A General linear model can be expressed by the following equation:
Yt = f(Xt, t; θ) + ϵt
(2.2)
Where:
• Yt is the output we aim to model
• Xt indicates the p independent variables Xt = (X1t, · · · , Xpt)T
• t is the time index
• θ indicates m unknown parameters (θ1, · · · , θm)T
• ϵt is a sequence of random variables with mean zero, variance σ2
t , and Cov[ϵti, ϵtj] = σ2
t Σij
For now we restrict the discussion to the case where Xt is non-random and thus we write xt instead
of Xt. Furthermore this general linear model expression also have a more general form, which can be
seen below:
Yt = xT
t θ + ϵt
(2.3)
where each letter are vectors including pairs of data except for the vectors of θ, such that it will look
like the following:



(2.4)
With this type of model both non and quadratic terms can be included like this example:


...

[1

(2.5)

(2.6)
The linearity in the parameters is very important. So the way that these constants of θ are found is by
the following equation, which requires that x has full rank.
ˆθ = (xT x)−1xT Y
(2.7)
This equation for the estimate θ that minimises the residual error: Y−Xθ is derived as following. First
we want to solve the equation which minimises the residual error:
∂

(2.8)

∥y −Xθ∥= (y −Xθ)T (y −Xθ)+

(2.9)

For the general linear model, it has the following assumptions to be met:
• The data points need to be independently distributed.
• The residuals are independent.
• The residuals are normally distributed.
• The residuals have constant variance.
If those four assumptions aren’t met at a certain level, the model is not very good for describing the
data points. Here a QQ-plot can be useful when deciding whether the residuals are normal distributed.
2.6.1
Prediction in the general linear model
So when the general linear model model is used to predict the users behaviour and grade, then the
known and estimated parameters are given with the following equations:
Known parameters:
ˆYt+ℓ= Eθ[Yt+ℓ|Xt+ℓ= xt+ℓ] = xT
t+ℓθ
(2.13)
Vθ[Yt+ℓ−ˆYt+ℓ] = Vθ[ϵt+ℓ] = σ2
(2.14)
Estimated parameters:
ˆYt+ℓ= Eˆθ[Yt+ℓ|Xt+ℓ= xt+ℓ] = xT
t+ℓˆθ
(2.15)
Vˆθ[Yt+ℓ−ˆYt+ℓ] = Vˆθ[ϵt+ℓ+ xT
t+ℓ(θˆθ)] = ˆσ2[1 + xT
t+ℓ(xT x)−1xt+ℓ]
(2.16)
But when we therefore want to predict the users behaviour and grade, we then have to use an estimate
of α. Therefore a 100(1 −α)% prediction interval of a future value is calculated as:
ˆYt+ℓ± tα/2(n −p)ˆσ2√
1 + xT
t+ℓ(xT x)−1xt+ℓ
(2.17)
where tα/2(n −p) refers to the α/2’th quantile of the t-distribution with n −p degrees of freedom. If
the value of n −p is large, then percentiles from the normal distribution can be used instead of the
t-distribution [Mad07].
==PAGE:24==
14
2 Article re-creation
2.6.2
Reasons for a general linear model might be inappropriate
The general linear model might be inappropriate. The reason is because we don’t want to predict the
amount of window openings that would occur in each dwelling and its rooms whenever it happens. We
rather want to predict the rate of opening or closing the windows given the opposite state, and not
number of outcome, such that the predicted outcome will be the probability of getting the window
opened or closed instead. Since we want to model the rate instead of the number window actions, then
a general linear model will be inappropriate for this analysis. Therefore a generalized linear model will
be used instead of a general linear model. Therefore for this binary case where a window can be either
open or closed, a logistic binomial regression will be used. This leads to the next section, decribing
logistic regression method followed by the generalized linear model.
2.6.3
Logistic Regression
In the following will the logistic regression be presented. The logistic regression is a useful tool for
transformation and can be applied e.g. in the following cases:
• To model the binary response variable as probabilities given the explanatory variables.
• When performing descriptive analyses between categories of groups.
• Predicting the probabilities of the responses given the data observations.
where the assumptions are:
• A linear relationship between the explanatory variables and the response variables are not as-
sumed.
• A linear relationship between the logistic transformation of the explanatory variables and the
response is assumed.
• Independent variables can be even the power terms or some other nonlinear transformations of
the original independent variables
• The binary logistic regression assume binomial distribution of the response. Therefore, the depen-
dent variable does not need to be normally distributed.
• The homogeneity of variance doesn’t need to be satisfied.
• The errors doesn’t need to be normally distributed but they have to be independent.
Given that the binary data observations of the window actions can be solved with logistic regression,
then the logistic regression can be used to determine the probability of the actions of closing of opening
the windows in a given dwelling.
The logistic regression method can investigate relationships between the explanatory variables and the
response. The prediction is the sum of the weighted contribution of the explanatory variables, and
the response is an estimated percentage of how likely an observation is to be classified to any class of
open or closed state of the window actions. For example, a simple logistic regression equation with one
explanatory variable is defined by the following formula:
Pr(x) =
exp (β0 + β1x)
1 + exp (β0 + β1x)
(2.18)
where we have the intercept β0 and the estimate β1. In cases where data sets have multiple variables
that are included in the model which are contributing, each of these i-variables will be assigned with
the estimates βi. In Equation 2.18 the formula has been linearized to the following:
log
(
Pr(x)
1 −Pr(x)
)
= β0 + β1x
(2.19)
where we can see that the relationship is defined as a linear function with the response term as a log-
odds value, and the estimates β which are estimated by the gradient descent algorithm where every
==PAGE:25==
2.7 Method: Generalized linear models
15
explanatory variables linear combinations are optimised for the odds value. These odds, as shown on
the left hand side of the above equation, are defined as the probability of an event divided by the
probability of the event not happening.
2.7
Method: Generalized linear models
When fitting a generalized linear model, we have to explain the models variables similar as in the
general linear model. A Generalized Linear Model will then be used for this as earlier mentioned, which
is defined as following in matrix form [Mad10]:
g(E[Y]) = Xβ
(2.20)
which is equals to:
g(E[Yi]) = β1xi1 + ... + βkxik
(2.21)
where the function g(.) is called the link function and the right hand side of (2.21) is called the linear
component of the model. Further the model contains a specification of the probability density of Y ,
which includes the Gaussian, Poisson, Binomial, Gamma, and other distributions. Another thing is the
smooth monotonic behaviour of the link function g(.). Here we have some freedom, but the so-called
canonical link function is directly linked to the used density.
Since that the project data response variable is binary classified, then it means that the initial model
will have the probability density of Y is set to the logistic binomial regression distribution and the
link function g(.) = logit(.) = ln
(
exp(βx)
1+exp(βx)
)
, where the assumptions are that all ϵi ∼N(0, σ2I) are
independent and that:
• The model structure should capture the systematic effects in the data.
• There are variance homogeneity of residuals.
• There are inndependence of residuals.
Therefore by sum up all the advantages of a generalized linear model compared to a traditional Ordinary
Least Square regression, we have the following pros mentioned from [18]:
• Transformation of the response variable Y to a normal distribution is not needed.
• The choice of link is separate from the choice of random component thus we have more flexibility
in modeling.
• If the link produces additive effects, then we do not need constant variance.
• The models are fitted via Maximum Likelihood estimation; thus optimal properties of the estima-
tors.
• All the inference tools and model checking for logistic regression models apply for other generalized
linear model too; e.g., Wald and Likelihood ratio tests, Deviance, Residuals, Confidence intervals,
Over-dispersion.
With this in mind the forward selection approach of finding the optimal generalized linear models will
be explained hereafter.
==PAGE:26==
16
2 Article re-creation
2.7.1
Forward selection
Figure 2.5: Flow diagram for Forward-selection proce-
dure in step-wise regression analysis from
the book [Bja07]
The forward selection approach is a method
for finding the next exploratory variable term
which is makes the model most significant com-
pared to the alternatives.
In this procedure
we start with the constant term in the equa-
tion only.
This means we only have the inter-
cept in our model which we thereafter add the
next variables as shown in the diagram.
We
therefore choose the exploratory variable which
shows the greatest correlation with the depen-
dent variable.
We then perform an F-test to
check if this coefficient is significantly differ-
ent from 0.
If so, then it is included in the
model. Among the exploratory variables not yet
included we now choose the one that has the
greatest (absolute) partial correlation coefficient
with the dependent variable given the variables
already in the equation. We perform an F-test
to check if the new variable has contributed to
the reduction of the residual variance, i.e.
if
the coefficient for it is different from 0.
If so,
continue as before.
If not, stop the analysis
[Bja07].
This approach is therefore simple and easy to apply since it add the variables with highest information
criterion one at a time. It is therefore good since only a reasonable number of models should be tested
and therefore can save calculation time. Further it can be used when we have more parameters then
observations, but it might not give the best combination of features [Tre09].
2.8
Results: Generalized linear models
In this section the found statistically results for the different binomial logistic generalized linear models
will be presented. The aim for this article recreation were to spot differences in the data sets defined
groups 1-4 from Table 2.2. This means that dependant of the dwelling were categories as rental or
owned, and has either natural or mechanical ventilation, 8 models are created by forward selection
using the Bayesian information criterion as value for acceptance of adding another variable. Further
two models for each group have been estimated, since the prediction of having a window open action
requires a data observation with closed windows and with closed and open windows, respectively, which
means the data have been split into subset when building the proposed models. At last if any models
included the variable for each index of any dwelling, then these variables which interact with the dwelling
index variable have been left out, since it will describe the individual dwelling behaviour in the models
which is not interesting for this goal and this gives a new model without interaction within dwellings.
Therefore the following sections will describe the found models and their statistically significance of
variables together with their estimates.
==PAGE:27==

The model intercept assumes that the ’TimeOfDay’ is ’afternoon’(13-18 o’clock), the room is ’bedroom’

another categorical state for example the time of day, e.g. to ’morning’ equals to 6-9 o’clock. To sum

and the concentration of CO2 decreases the probability of opening the windows when the dwellings are
owner-occupied with natural ventilation. In other ways, the occupants’ opening behaviours are more
likely to happen in bedrooms than living rooms for these groups 1 dwellings. So to sum it up it can be
seen that the three continuous variables: CO2 concentration, Indoor temperature and Solar radiation
have an statistically significant positive effect for the probability of window opening action, which were
likely to expect, since the appearance of sun light, higher temperature and CO2 filled air is what could
be expected to be reasons for occupants’ window opening actions. At last we note that some ’levels’ of
the categorical variable ’TimeOfDay’ aren’t significant at a level of 5%.

2.8.5
Summary of the groups results
Given all the found results a summary of the four groups results for each of the cases of window opening
action or window closing action can be summed up. In the above models, patterns in the expected be-
haviours have been found, but also that some variable appeared more often for models when predicting
occupants’ window opening behaviours, and other variables were seen for predicting occupants’ window
closing behaviours. Most commonly for the models predicting window opening actions, were because
of the increase of the CO2 concentration level. This variable has a positive estimate and therefore
affecting the model the most in general. For the case of closing window, the outdoor temperature was
also frequently included with a negative coefficient to explain that higher temperatures lead to lower
probability of closing dwellings windows.
At last also interaction within the dwelling index variable were seen in the R coding, and thereafter left
out due to this article aim of focusing on the groups and not the individually dwellings. This means
that these defined groups are not perfectly well describing the data observation for each dwelling and
how the occupants’ behave differently from each other. Therefore the next chapter will introduce to
the mixed effect models, which can deal with exactly this type of problem.
==PAGE:36==
26
==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER 3
Generalized linear mixed
effects models
In the following mixed effects models will be introduced. Mixed-effects contains two elements called
random and fixed effects. The fixed effect are similar to the earlier mentioned estimates from previously
GLM models, but the term of the random effects changes the logistic function (given the case of
an binomial distributed data set). It moves its position and slope, which affect the likelihood of the
explanatory variables. This can be visualised in Figure 3.1 found in the following link [19]. This method
can be applied as an extension of the earlier mentioned binomial logistic regression, which gives the
following form of the mixed-effects binomial logistic regression which furthermore has advantages for
data modelling. Among these are the following advantages over simpler statistical tests:
• Mixed-effects models are multivariate models.
• Mixed models allow to statistically incorporate within-dwelling variability.
• Mixed-models provide a wealth of diagnostic statistics.
Thus, it means that it is possible to control multicollinearity and correlations between explanatory
variables. In addition, since it can be interpreted within groups, it then works great for hierarchical
data structures even though it is nested. Furthermore, explanatory variables effect can be tested simul-
taneously.
Figure 3.1: The binomial logistic mixed-effect model possibility
to capture the data
==PAGE:38==
28
3 Generalized linear mixed effects models
In the figure above, the effect of using the mixed effect model advantages can be seen. Here it shows the
use of only fixed effects, fixed effects and a random term(e.g. each of the dwellings), fixed effects and a
random term slope for one variable and all of it combined giving a model with flexibility to capture the
data structure depending on the different groups, e.g. dwelling. With this in mind we have a reason to
use another method for the handling of the dwelling data, and this will be described hereafter.
3.1
Method: Generalized linear mixed effects models
In this section the generalized linear mixed effect model will be presented, which in general is defined
identical as the generalized linear model, but also includes the random effect term. In the book in
[Wu09] Definition 5.3 is the definition of the model explicit written as following:
Y = Xβ + ZU + ϵ
(3.1)
with X and Z denoting known matrices, and where ϵ ∼N(0, Σ) and U ∼N(0, Ψ) are independent is
called a mixed general linear model. In the general case the covariance matrices Σ and Ψ may depend
on some unknown parameters, Ψ, that also need to be estimated. The parameters β are called fixed
effects or systematic effects, while the quantities U are called random effects.
The estimation of fixed effects and variance parameters are given by the formulas from chapter 5.4 in
[Wu09]:
(
XT V −1X
)
β = XT V −1y
(3.2)
β =
(
XT V −1X
)−1
XT V −1y
(3.3)
Var[ˆβ] =
(
XT V −1X
)−1
(3.4)
and the estimation of random effects is given by the formula:
(
ZT Σ−1Z + Ψ−1)
u = ZT Σ−1(y −Xβ)
(3.5)
u =
(
ZT Σ−1Z + Ψ−1)−1
ZT Σ−1(y −Xβ)
(3.6)
In the following section the Laplace approximation will be presented as one of the methods to deal with
nonlinear and non-normal mixed effects models.
3.1.1
Laplace approximation
In general, numerical methods must be used since it will be impossible to obtain closed form solutions
for nonlinear and non-normal mixed effects models. Therefore to deal with this, the estimation will be
based on likelihood principles and the general mixed effects model can be seen below by its likelihood
function as following formula:
LM(θ; y) =
∫
Rq L(θ; u, y)du
(3.7)
where y is the observed random variables, θ is the model parameters to be estimated, and U is the
q unobserved random variables or effects as explained by the book [Mad10]. Furthermore, the joint
likelihood of both the observed and the unobserved random variables is here formulated as the likeli-
hood function L. The likelihood function for estimating θ is the marginal likelihood LM obtained by
==PAGE:39==
3.1 Method: Generalized linear mixed effects models
29
integrating out the unobserved random variables.
Since the integral in Equation 3.7 generally is difficult to solve, it therefore needs some rewriting to
apply the numerical solution. The reason the integral is computational heavy to solve is due to cases
where we have many number of q, the unobserved random variables. In a example with large values of
q it can be seen that the computational demands increase significantly due to the product rule.
So since the only assumption for applying the mixed modeling is to define a joint likelihood function for
a model, then the likelihood function in Equation 3.7 can be used as a definition of mixed models. With
this in mind it means that given any likelihood based statistical modeling we can use mixed modeling.
Therefore with the above Equation 3.7 it can be specified below, with the total set of parameters as
θ = (β, Ψ), which gives the following formula:
LM(β, Ψ; y) =
∫
Rq L(β, Ψ; u, y)du
(3.8)
Since the integral in Equation 3.8 still is generally difficult to solve even with the parameter change,
the Laplace approximation can be applied as described in chapter 5.10 in the book [Mad10].
For a given set of parameters in the given model, θ, the joint log-likelihood ℓ(θ, u, y) = log(L(θ, u, y)) is
approximated by a second order Taylor approximation around the optimum u = buθ of the log-likelihood
function w.r.t. the unobserved random variables u, as seen in Equation 3.9, where the optimum is u
and the negative Hessian of the joint log-likelihood H(u) = −ℓ′′
uu(θ, u, y)|u=¯u is evaluated at ˜u.
ℓ(θ, u, y) ≈ℓ(θ, ˜u, y) −1
2(u −˜u)T H(˜u)(u −˜u)
(3.9)
Using the approximation from Equation 3.9 and Equation 3.8, it can be seen that the Laplace approx-
imation of the marginal log-likelihood can be reformulated such that the integral can be replaced by
reformulation the equation. This gives a term for which the numerical solution can be found.

3 Generalized linear mixed effects models
3.2
Results: Generalized linear mixed effects models
In this section the found statistically results for the different binomial logistic mixed effect models will
be presented. In the article recreation result section it was mentioned that the interaction within the
dwelling index variable was seen as significant in the R coding. Thus, it was left out due to this articles
aim of focusing on the groups and not the individually dwellings. Therefore, since the mixed effect
models can deal with exactly this type of problem, two mixed effect models for either predicting the
occupants’ window opening or closing behaviours will be estimated and shown. This means that each
dwelling individual effect will be included as a random term to the mixed effect model and hopefully
being able to explain the occupants’ behaviours which might be different from each other.
Furthermore, a Fourier series have been added optionally variables to include, for which the Fourier
series is used to describe the data observations hour variable with the possibility of having up to a
fourth order expansion. This given Fourier series with T-periodic functions can be used to model with
this T-periodic seasonality, based on Appendix C in [Chr12] where for a given number T > 0 we can
set ω = 2π/T, such that cos(nωx), sin(nωx) will be used to define the Fourier series.
The two models for each window action prediction have been estimated by forward selection using
the Bayesian information criterion as value for acceptance of adding another significant variable. The
prediction of having a window opening action requires a data observation with closed windows and
vice versa, which means the data have been split into subset when building the proposed models.
Furthermore, all continuously variables have been adjusted with their own mean values, such that
they become centred around zero giving a well defined model with a better intercept. Therefore in
the following sections they will be described together with their statistically significance of variables
together with their estimates.

relative humidity and solar radiation are also affecting in same direction which in a general point of view

increases.

The calculation time
In the following section three R coding methods have been used for evaluating the calculation speed
of each mixed effect model (MEM) found by the forward selection, since they use different numeri-
cal approximation methods for estimation parameters coefficients. For this testing the ”glmmTMB”,
”glmer0” and ”glmer1” have been used where the zero and one for ’glmer’ is indicating whether it uses
the Laplace approximation or the adaptive Gauss-Hermite approximation to the log-likelihood. These

much faster than both of the others when the increase of variables, both fixed and random terms are

of the mixed effect modelling, it is suggested to use ’glmmTMB’ for estimating mixed effect models due
to is speed and still gave exact same results for the models parameters when compared to the others
results.
3.3.1
Summary of the mixed effect models results
Based on the found results, a summary of the two binomial logistic mixed effect models for respectively
the cases of window opening action and window closing action can be made. In the above models,
patterns in the expected behaviours have been found. Also that some variables appeared more often
for models when predicting occupants’ window opening behaviours, and other variables were seen for
predicting occupants’ window closing behaviours. Most important for the models prediction window
opening action was the increase of CO2 concentration level due to the variables higher positive estimate
for its coefficient in combination with the other positive variables; indoor temperature and solar radi-
ation with lower coefficients. For the other case of closing windows, the outdoor temperature variable
were also included with a negative coefficient to explain that higher temperatures outside lead to lower
the probability of closing dwellings windows. Apart from that, the wind speed also had a significantly
positive effect of increasing the probability for closing windows in any dwelling whenever the wind speed
increases, which haven’t been seen before in this project or the article [And+13].
Further, the interaction within the dwelling index as the random effect were found to have significant
effect in the models since some dwellings e.g. number 1 and 7 implied more window actions for both
cases. Especially for window opening it was found that the CO2 effect had a high standard deviation,
and from the Figure 3.2 the CO2 effect varies a lot depending the individually dwellings meaning that
CO2 influence the occupants window opening behaviour significantly. This really shows how the oc-
cupants’ behave differently from each other in their respective dwellings and the mixed effect model
improved the explanation of the data observation by including the random effect terms to the remaining
fixed effects.
In additional, Fourier series with cosines and sinus terms have also played a role for estimating the
variable for the observed hour, which takes into account the fluctuations that may be related to its
periodic seasonality.
The data have been measured every 10 minutes intervals, where the next observation is dependent on
the previously and the response variable are observed as discrete states, time series method can be
applied instead. Therefore the next chapter will introduce to the hidden Markov model and its Markov
chain model, which can deal with exactly this type of problem, where different states and changes
between these states can be explained by the model in order to conclude new results.
==PAGE:50==
40
==PAGE:51==
CHAPTER 4
Hidden Markov model
The Hidden Markov models are based on the process of the unobserved parameter {Ct : t = 1, 2, ...}
which have to satisfy the defined Markov property mentioning later and also the process of the state
dependency {Xt : t = 1, 2, ...}. The Hidden Markov model is a dependent model which is based on a
distribution which generates observations that are depended on the mixtures modelled of an underlying
and unobserved Markov chain. These models are well performing under both univariate and multivari-
ate time series data observation including both categorical and discrete values for the series of observed
data. The Hidden Markov models are useful in this project, since they allow each of the observations
probability distribution to be depended on the hidden states of the Markov chain. Furthermore this
implies that it can meet both the serial dependence in data and overdispersion, where the statistical
model expected variance is lower then the presence in the data set. In the following the method of a
Hidden Markov model will described from the book [Wal09] be explained.
The Markov chain can therefore be displayed in the following directed graph in Figure 4.1 which the
past and the future are dependent only through the present observation.
Markov process :
Ct−1
Ct
Ct+1
· · ·
CT −1
Observations :
Xt−1
Xt
Xt+1
Figure 4.1: The directed graph for a Markov chain
4.1
Method: Hidden Markov model
First a visual graphic example of the Markov chain for the data sets states: Open and Closed windows
is visualised in Figure 4.2 below, where the transition probabilities for staying in the same states or
changing to the other is represented by γ11 and γ2.
Open
Closed
γ11
(1 −γ11)
γ22
(1 −γ22)
Figure 4.2: Markov chain between window states with transition
probabilities γ11 and γ22
==PAGE:52==
42
4 Hidden Markov model
When we want to apply such Markov chain with a switching behaviour in these window states, it then
also has to follow the assumptions in which the data follows a Markov process that undergoes suddenly
changes from one state to another.
So as the definition says, then a sequence of discrete random
variables {Ct : t ∈N} is a Markov chain if it satisfies the Markov Property:
Pr (Ct+1|Ct, . . . , C1) = Pr (Ct+1|Ct)
(4.1)
Furthermore the earlier mentioned transition probability, γij, from a given state i to j is defined as the
conditional probability:
γij(t) = Pr (Cs+t = j|Cs = i)
(4.2)
where the transition probability matrix, Γ(t), is defined by the individual transition probabilities of the
switching behaviours, such that each row sums to 1 = 100%.
Γ =



γ11
· · ·
γ1m
...
...
...
γm1
· · ·
γmm



(4.3)
A Markov Chain is only said to be homogeneous if it is independent on the value (s) equal to the time.
Furthermore, if the transition probability matrix Γ(t) is a stationary distribution, then it needs to have
a initial distribution δ with a row vector with non negative elements such that the following applies:
Γδ = δ and δ1′ = 1.
Since the given data set for this thesis have an switching behaviour between the two states Open and
Closed as shown in Figure 4.2, then a homogeneous 2 × 2 dimensional transition probability matrix is
defined as below in Equation 4.4. This matrix uses the Markov Chains transition probabilities whenever
a change in states changes to another. These states are therefore ”hidden”. The transition probabilities
in each state are therefore leading the way for the probability distribution that calculates the given
observation in same observed time, which then is only dependant on the given state for the Markov
chain {Ct} shown in Equation 4.5.
Γ =

γ11
1 −γ11
1 −γ22
γ22
)
(4.4)
Pr

Xt|X(t−1), C(t))
= Pr (Xt|Ct)
(4.5)
So in an given case where we look into the observations which are discrete, then the found probabilities
in the above Equation 4.5 will be equals to the distribution of state {Ct} probability mass function.
pi(x) = Pr (Xt = x|Ct = i)
(4.6)
Given the earlier mentioned equations, we have the now defined that the Equation 4.1 is the describing
the state and also that the Equation 4.6 is the equation describing the observation. Therefore together
with the Hidden Markov model which is defined as a state-space model, then we also need the assumption
that the state-space is finite. In addition, it can also be mentioned that state-space is not necessary
perfectly separable. Therefore it can be seen that in any state-space where the conditional distributions
have overlaps, the observations may arise from more than one state. With this knowledge is it possible
given the observations to estimate the hidden state {Ct}. Therefore to expand this model, another
Markov chain {Ct} with m-states could be considered with the following where the logistic binomial
regression is used.
Pr (Ct = j|Ct−1 = i) = γij
(4.7)

Given a Fourier series with T-periodic functions, it can be illustrated how a model with this T-periodic
seasonality would look like. This is shown in Appendix C in [Chr12] where for a given number T > 0
we can set ω = 2π/T, such that cos(nωx), sin(nωx) will be used to define the Fourier series.
logitγij = βij1 + βij2 cos(2π/T) + βij2 sin(2π/T)
(4.10)
At last, some thoughts have been mentioned from the text written in [Jua86], where a definition for the
Hidden Markov model is defined. Here it is stated that the Hidden Markov model should include three
fundamental problems (Likelihood, Decoding and Learning) as stated below:
• Problem 1: Given parameters θ of the observation sequence X(T ), decide the likelihood.
• Problem 2: Decide the most optimal hidden state sequence C(T ) of an observation sequence X(T )
given the parameters θ.
• Problem 3: Given the found information in the previously, we can compute the parameters θ in
the Hidden Markov model.
4.1.1
Gradient Descent algorithm
Figure 4.3: The iterative path of the Gradient Descent
algorithm to find the local or global mini-
mum
Louis Augustin Cauchy, the French mathemati-
cian
invented
in
1847
the
so
called
Gradi-
ent Descent algorithm [Gut18].
The Gradi-
ent Descent algorithm is used whenever the
minimum of a given object function needs to
be found such that its results can be opti-
mised.
The reason to use this algorithm or
similar optimising methods is, when the ana-
lytical solution can’t be found for the given
problem that should be optimised.
Therefore
a numerically approach can be used to solve
it.
The Gradient Descent algorithm will therefore be
used for minimizing, given a function that can cal-
culate the negative log likelihood and then find
the most optimal estimates in the given data
space. Therefore through interactive steps we can
find the search direction with the largest negative
gradient that minimises the fastest given the func-
tion f(x). The algorithm benefits are that no linear algebra is involved and we need no second derivative,
because the search direction is computed by ∇f(x). This makes it easy to implement, but the disad-
vantages is that the convergence can be very slow compared to other similar complex algorithm for
minimizing.
The algorithm inputs are a function, the models estimates and response variable. The outputs are
the converged point, which means the found solution for the models estimates together with the found
==PAGE:54==
44
4 Hidden Markov model
minimised object value of the negative log likelihood and the information of the convergence, number
of iterations. The example of the code, can be found in Listing 3.
The pseudo code below in algorithm 1 show the input and output parameters need to apply the Gradient
Descent algorithm. The input values will in this case be the window states as the response Y , where the
other inputs are initialisation of variables and parameters θ, X and the stopping criteria for tolerance,
iterations.
Algorithm 1 Gradient Descent
input : Y , θ, X, η, tolerance, max iterations
Result: θ
1 for i = 0; i < max iterations; i + + do
2
Costi = f(X, Y )θ
3
if ∥Costi −Costi1∥< tolerance then
4
break
5
else
6
∇f(X, Y )θ =
∂
∂θf(X, Y )
7
for j = 1; i < length θ; j + + do
8
θj = θj −η · ∇f(X, Y )θj
9
end
10
end
11 end
The algorithm stopping criteria is a check if it is converged and the number of iterations have been
reached. If not so, then it computes the search direction and given that direction, the ”line search” al-
gorithm, tries to find the best possible step length. The line search method computes a search direction
and then decides how far to move along that direction.
In the following citation: [Hoe14] Jennifer Hoeting present the nlminb() R-function and how to apply
it:
nlminb(start, objective, control, lower, upper)
nlminb() takes a function, objective, and finds values for the parameters of this function at
which the objective function achieves its minimum value. Unlike optimize(), nlminb() can
be used to optimize functions with multiple arguments. nlminb() also cannot be used to find
a point which maximizes the objective function, so to find the maximum, we must minimize
the negative objective function. Based on some testing, it appears the convergence order is
super linear and close to 2.
The start argument is a vector of starting points for each of the parameters of the objective
function.
control is a list of control parameters such as the maximum number of iterations (iter.max)
and relative tolerance (rel.tol), which defaults to 1e-10.
lower and upper are the bounds for constrained optimization, which can be especially useful
if there are many local minimums. lower is negative infinity by default and upper is infinity
by default.
nlminb() returns a list with six arguments. par is (are) the value(s) of the parameter(s) that
optimize(s) the objective function. objective is the the value of the objective function evaluated
at the arguments which optimize it. convergence, if 0, indicated a successful convergence.
iterations is the number of iterations needed to achieve absolute convergence. evaluations
tells the number of times the objective function and its derivative were evaluated. Finally,
message gives some additional information, often displaying the number of iterations needed
to achieve relative convergence.
==PAGE:55==

opt <- nlminb(params,nll.fun,X_O=X_O,X_C=X_C,Gamma=Gamma,y_t=y_t,control=list(trace=1))

4.2
Results: Hidden Markov model
In this section, the analysed results based on the R coding from listing 20. Here several found models
given the chosen dwelling can be seen in the R code in listing 22 where the use of the Hidden Markov
model results will be presented. Since the data have been measured in every 10 minutes intervals, where
the next observation is dependent on the previously and the response variable are observed as discrete
states, time series methods can be applied instead. The advantage of using this kind of method is due
to it depend on the past information and not the future, which in the window modelling case make
sense, since any action of opening or closing rarely happens for each 10 minutes intervals. Therefore
since windows often will stay in same state for longer intervals the reason for using this models will be
an advantage, where different states and changes between these states can be explained by the model
in order to conduct result, which will be differently from the mixed effect models and generalized linear
model resulting models.
The Hidden Markov model assumes that data must be a time series therefore means that the found
models only needs data from a single dwelling in a single room such that every observation follows
the each others in a 10 min interval without jump in time, e.g. if different rooms or dwellings where
included. This model should therefore be able to predict the occupants’ window opening behaviours.
==PAGE:56==
46
4 Hidden Markov model
The R coding for Markov Chain function can be seen in listing 4 below which were used to calculate
the negative log likelihood for the model followed by the optimization of the parameters estimates as
mentioned in subsection 4.1.1. The Gradient Descent algorithm finds the optimal minimum based on
the negative log likelihood and hereby gives the models parameter estimates.
1
nll.fun <- function(params,X_O,X_C,Gamma,y_t){
2
n <- dim(X_C)[1]
3
# Define parameters
4
beta_O <- params[1:dim(X_O)[2]]
5
beta_C <- params[(dim(X_O)[2]+1):length(params)]
6
7
# Calculate gamma_11
8
temp <- X_O %*% beta_O
9
gamma_21 <- (exp(temp))/(1+exp(temp))
10
11
# Calculate gamma_22
12
temp <- X_C %*% beta_C
13
gamma_12 <- (exp(temp))/(1+exp(temp))
14
15
# Initialize log-Likelihood
16
ll <- 0
17
for (t in 1:(n-1)) {
18
# Update transition probability matrix
19
Gamma[1,1] <- 1-gamma_12[t]
20
Gamma[1,2] <- gamma_12[t]
21
Gamma[2,1] <- gamma_21[t]
22
Gamma[2,2] <- 1-gamma_21[t]
23
24
# Calculate next step, u_(t+1)
25
u_t1 <- y_t[t,] %*% Gamma
26
# Loglikelihood
27
ll <- ll+log(sum(u_t1*y_t[t+1,]))
28
}
29
-ll
30
}

4.2.2
Summary of the hidden Markov model and its Markov chain results
The results and facts found from the hidden Markov model and its Markov chain have been focusing on
a single dwelling impact on window opening behaviours, where optimization with the Gradient descent
method is used. This ensures optimal estimates for the parameters are obtained through calculation,
where the Markov transitions probabilities are updated in the Markov process matrix. Based on the
founds models for dwelling 1, predictions have been performed to show their individually influence for
occupants’ window opening behaviours in dwelling 1 living room and bedroom independent on each
other such that observed data have been approaches as time series for each prediction. From these plots
the differences in the effect could be seen based on how much the probability for a window action were
influenced. In the living rooms prediction the indoor temperature were showing huge impact on window
opening actions if the temperature went beyond 25 degrees. Further when changing the parameter for
CO2 instead of having it set to mean value as previously, it was seen that changes for CO2 when
predicting the opening behaviours given the indoor temperature gave significantly different prediction.
This were shown in Figure 4.7. Whereas if the other variables: Solar radiation or outdoor temperature
didn’t influence the prediction when changing these parameters instead of setting them to theirs mean
value.
==PAGE:64==
54
==PAGE:65==
CHAPTER 5
Discussion
In the statistical analysis conducted through chapter 2, 3 and 4, where different modellings have
been applied in order to achieve a well describing regression model for window opening behaviours,
assumption about the occupant have been stated. It is assumed that the occupants’ are representative
for theirs given dwelling cf. Table 2.1, where building year and possible renovation, floor area and level
including the possibility of being a smoker are some of the stated information. Assumptions about
the variables are also stated, but for these, changes can be described mathematically with laws of
nature, whereas occupants’ and human beings are variables that don’t behave normally as expected.
This is e.g. in situation where people get sick, stressed or are occupied with other work, which affect
their window behaviours. Since the data doesn’t describe the occupants level of personal behaviours
which could be categories e.g. as normal/sick/occupied, then false behaviours for any of the occupants
window actions can be miss leading due to the mentioned arguments.
Furthermore another point of view could be when external influences, such as political, health and
energy proposals, have an impact on residents’ behaviour, thus the model no longer describes the
occupants’ behaviours. Other examples could also be due to global or national crisis as air pollution,
epidemic outbreak, war or weather condition which prevent occupants from their usual behaviours and
again leading to another resulting data measurement.
In addition, when speaking of the data measurements, then in a supervising meeting, we have talked
about the influences of indoor climate when a closed room opens its door to other air quality. From
this discussion it was stated that e.g. a decrease in the CO2 concentration could both occur when
opening windows but also when opening doors to other areas, such that the air could mix and transfer
with other indoor climate environments. This can give data observation miss leading values for the
independent variables relative to the value describing the window actions. At last it have been stated in
the original article from 2013, [And+13], that the measurement only took place in the ’main’ living and
bedroom, even though when the dwellings included several more windows. Therefore the occupants’
were told to open the ’main’ windows whenever they opened windows next to it. This could therefore
lead to measurement errors, because if the occupants’ forgot opening the corresponding main window,
then the measured data in data sets would show changes in variables without any window action.
Therefore to quote the original article [And+13], it were written the following:
This classification was based on a questionnaire survey conducted by Andersen et al.
who found that the statement ”I had to leave the dwelling” was mentioned amongst the most
common reasons for closing windows.
This show that window closing actions were influenced by the occupants behaviours also describing
their activity further from their respond to indoor climate and air quality. With this and the other
mentioned argument describing possibilities for occupants behaviours and optional changes, then it can
be justified why occupants actions are difficult to explain to perfection.
==PAGE:66==
56
==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 6
Conclusion
The aim of this report has been to develop a statistical model that can describe the behaviour of
the occupants concerning opening and closing windows in different types of dwellings in Denmark.
A precise model of this will assist architects and building engineers in the future to design Danish
dwellings that has a better energy saving without changing the general behaviour of the occupants
living in these types of dwellings.
The work in this report is based on a huge amount of data gathered from a previous study from
2013 which is described in a paper with the following title: “Window opening behaviour modelled
from measurements in Danish dwellings”, written by civil engineers from Denmark and Italy.
The
measurement data has been provided by my supervisors at DTU Compute and DTU Byg for further
analysis in this project by using more complex mathematical and statistical analysis methods. While
the previous work was based on a relative simple Generalized Linear Regression model, and had focus
on the types of dwellings, grouped into four factual types of ownership and mechanical ventilation
installations, this work is also considering the behaviour of the occupants concerning opening and
closing the windows in the specific dwellings.
In the first part of this report the model used in the previous work from 2013 has been developed in
order to recreate and verify the previous results concerning window opening and closing actions. It was
demonstrated that the model’s patterns in the expected behaviours were exact to the results described
in the article.
It was also found that some variables appeared more often for models predicting
occupants’ window opening behaviours, while other variables were seen for predicting occupants’
window closing behaviours. In general, the probability of the window opening actions increased with
the amount of the CO2 concentration level. Exact as in the article, this variable were found as the main
parameter affecting the window opening models. For the case of closing windows, it was determine that
the outdoor temperature was significant with a negative coefficient to explain that higher temperatures
lead to lower probability of closing dwellings windows, exactly as described in the article.
Finally, by recreating the article using R coding, it was concluded that there was a considerable
interaction within the dwelling index variables, i.e. within the groups of dwellings. This dependence
was considered out of scope and not taken into account in the models describing each groups window
behaviours, as in the article. Thus, it has been found that these models for the four defined groups are
not perfectly well describing the data observations for each dwelling and that the individual occupants
behave differently from each other.
In order to take into account this issue, a Mixed Effect model has been developed. Two binomial logistic
mixed effect models for respectively the cases of window opening action and window closing action
have been made. As in the previous model, patterns in the expected behaviours were found. It was also
observed, as expected, that some variable appeared more often for models predicting occupants’ window
opening behaviours, while other variables were seen for predicting occupants’ window closing behaviours.
The model prediction for window opening action showed a probability that increased with the CO2
concentration level due to the variables high positive estimate for its coefficient in combination with
the other positive variables: indoor temperature and solar radiation with lower coefficients. For the
==PAGE:68==
58
6 Conclusion
other case of closing windows, it was found that the outdoor temperature variable was significant with
a negative coefficient that explained that higher temperatures outside lead to a lower probability of
closing dwellings windows.
In addition, it was also found that the wind speed also had a significantly positive effect of increasing
the probability for closing windows in any dwelling whenever the wind speed increased, which haven’t
been observed before in this project or in the article [And+13].
The interaction within the dwelling index as the random effect were found to have significant effect in
the models, since especially dwelling number 1 and 7 both had more window actions compared to the
other dwellings. Specifically, for window opening it was found that the CO2 effect had a high standard
deviation and it varies a lot depending the individual dwellings, meaning that there is a significant
CO2 influence on the occupants window opening behaviour. The mixed effect model clearly showed
how the occupants behave differently from each other in their respective dwellings, and how the data
observations much better can be explained by including the random effect terms to the remaining fixed
effects.
Finally, Fourier series with cosines and sinus terms have been included for estimating the variable for the
observed hour, which takes into account the fluctuations that could be related to its periodic seasonality.
In the last part of the report, the measured data that all have been gathered with 10 minutes intervals,
are processed using a Hidden Markov model and its Markov Chain model.
By such a time series
method, the response variables are observed as discrete states, and the next data observation is
dependent on the previous, so different states and changes between these states can be explained by
the model in order to obtain even more precise results.
The results found from the hidden Markov model and its Markov chain have been optimised with
the Gradient descent method and has focused on a single dwelling impact on window opening
behaviours.
Predictions were performed to show their individual influence for occupants’ window
opening behaviours in dwelling 1 living room and bedroom independent on each other such that
observed data have been approaches as time series for each prediction. From the model we could see
differences in the effect based on how much the probability for a window action was influenced. In
the living room predictions, the indoor temperature showed a huge impact on window actions if the
temperature went beyond 25 degrees.
Further when changing the parameter for CO2 instead of having it set to mean value as previously, it
was seen that changes for CO2 when predicting the opening behaviours given the indoor temperature
gave significantly different prediction.
Whereas if the other variables: Solar radiation or outdoor
temperature didn’t influence the prediction when changing these parameters instead of setting them to
theirs mean value.
In total, we can conclude, that a mixed effect models and a Markov chain model with statistical
significant parameter and random effect for both window actions have been estimated by forward
selection. Theses models extension of the articles generalized linear model deals with dwelling indexes
and therefore capture the data structure and the occupants behaviours, such that it can be used for
buildings engineers simulations when testing new designs energy loss compared to indoor climate and
air quality.

Listing 20: The R coding for apply Markov Chains
1
nll.fun <- function(params,X_O,X_C,Gamma,y_t){
2
n <- dim(X_C)[1]
3
# Define parameters
4
beta_O <- params[1:dim(X_O)[2]]
5
beta_C <- params[(dim(X_O)[2]+1):length(params)]
6
7
# Calculate gamma_11
8
temp <- X_O %*% beta_O
9
gamma_21 <- (exp(temp))/(1+exp(temp))
10
11
# Calculate gamma_22
12
temp <- X_C %*% beta_C
13
gamma_12 <- (exp(temp))/(1+exp(temp))
14
15
# Initialize log-Likelihood
16
ll <- 0
17
for (t in 1:(n-1)) {
18
# Update transition probability matrix
19
Gamma[1,1] <- 1-gamma_12[t]
20
Gamma[1,2] <- gamma_12[t]
21
Gamma[2,1] <- gamma_21[t]
22
Gamma[2,2] <- 1-gamma_21[t]
23
24
# Calculate next step, u_(t+1)
25
u_t1 <- y_t[t,] %*% Gamma
26
# Loglikelihood
27
ll <- ll+log(sum(u_t1*y_t[t+1,]))
28
}
29
-ll
30
}

# Back transformation functions
3
# (Normalized and Scaled)
4

5
bt <- function(yt){
6
y.bt <- (exp(yt)/(exp(yt)+1))
7
return(list(y.l=y.bt))
8
}
